   648	
   649	I. Introduction
   650	
   651	Table of Contents
   652	1. Introduction
   653	
   654	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   655	
   656	Chapter 1. Introduction
   657	
   658	Introduction
   659	
   660	This is the Linux Standard Base Work In Progress 20030624 . An implementation
   661	of anything in this document may not claim to be an implementation of the Linux
   662	Standard Base until such time as the feature moves into the Linux Standards
   663	Base Specification.
   664	
   665	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   666	
   667	Purpose
   668	
   669	The Linux Standard Base (LSB) defines a system interface for compiled
   670	applications and a minimal environment for support of installation scripts. Its
   671	purpose is to enable a uniform industry standard environment for high-volume
   672	applications conforming to the LSB.
   673	
   674	The LSB defines a binary interface for application programs that are compiled
   675	and packaged for LSB-conforming implementations on many different hardware
   676	architectures. Since a binary specification must include information specific
   677	to the computer processor architecture for which it is intended, it is not
   678	possible for a single document to specify the interface for all possible
   679	LSB-conforming implementations. Therefore, the LSB is a family of
   680	specifications, rather than a single one.
   681	
   682	The LSB is composed of two basic parts: A common specification ("generic LSB"
   683	or "gLSB") describing those parts of the interface that remain constant across
   684	all implementations of the LSB, and an architecture-specific specification
   685	("archLSB") describing the parts of the interface that vary by processor
   686	architecture. Together, the generic LSB and the architecture-specific
   687	supplement for a single hardware architecture provide a complete interface
   688	specification for compiled application programs on systems that share a common
   689	hardware architecture.
   690	
   691	This document is the generic LSB. It must be used in conjunction with an
   692	architecture-specific supplement. Whenever a section of this specification must
   693	be supplemented by architecture-specific information, this document includes a
   694	reference to the architecture supplement. Architecture supplements may also
   695	contain additional information that is not referenced here.
   696	
   697	This document should be used in conjunction with the documents it references.
   698	This document enumerates the system components it includes, but descriptions of
   699	those components may be included entirely or partly in this document, partly in
   700	other documents, or entirely in other reference documents. For example, the
   701	section that describes system service routines includes a list of the system
   702	routines supported in this interface, formal declarations of the data
   703	structures they use that are visible to applications, and a pointer to the
   704	underlying referenced specification for information about the syntax and
   705	semantics of each call. Only those routines not described in standards
   706	referenced by this document, or extensions to those standards, are described in
   707	the detail. Information referenced in this way is as much a part of this
   708	document as is the information explicitly included here.
   709	
   710	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   711	
   712	Related Standards
   713	
   714	The specifications listed below are referenced in whole or in part by the Linux
   715	Standard Base. When a specification is referenced in a way that imposes a
   716	requirement (for example, "foo must behave as specified in the XyzzySpec"),
   717	then the relevant requirements of that specification apply as if they were part
   718	of the LSB itself. However, if the LSB refers to a specification without
   719	imposing a requirement, then it is merely a reference and does not add
   720	additional requirements. The LSB may refer to a portion of a specification (for
   721	example, to define a specific function or group of functions); in such cases,
   722	only the explicitly referenced portion of the specification applies.
   723

   840	+-----------------------------------------------------------------------------+
   841	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   842	
   843	Relevant Libraries
   844	
   845	The libraries listed here shall be available on a Linux Standard Base system.
   846	This list may be supplemented or amended by the architecture-specific
   847	specifications.
   848	
   849	Table 1-2. Standard Libraries Names
   850	
   851	+---------------------------+
   852	|Library   |Runtime Name    |
   853	|----------+----------------|
   854	|libX11    |libX11.so.6     |
   855	|----------+----------------|
   856	|libXt     |libXt.so.6      |
   857	|----------+----------------|
   858	|libGL     |libGL.so.1      |
   859	|----------+----------------|
   860	|libXext   |libXext.so.6    |
   861	|----------+----------------|
   862	|libICE    |libICE.so.6     |
   863	|----------+----------------|
   864	|libSM     |libSM.so.6      |
   865	|----------+----------------|
   866	|libdl     |libdl.so.2      |
   867	|----------+----------------|
   868	|libcrypt  |libcrypt.so.1   |
   869	|----------+----------------|
   870	|libz      |libz.so.1       |
   871	|----------+----------------|
   872	|libncurses|libncurses.so.5 |
   873	|----------+----------------|
   874	|libutil   |libutil.so.1    |
   875	|----------+----------------|
   876	|libpthread|libpthread.so.0 |
   877	|----------+----------------|
   878	|libstdcxx |libstdc++.so.5  |
   879	|----------+----------------|
   880	|libpam    |libpam.so.0     |
   881	|----------+----------------|
   882	|libgcc_s  |libgcc_s.so.1   |
   883	+---------------------------+
   884	
   885	Table 1-3. Standard Library Names defined in the Architecture Specific
   886	Supplement
   887	
   888	+------------------------+
   889	|Library   |Runtime Name |
   890	|----------+-------------|
   891	|libm      |See archLSB  |
   892	|----------+-------------|
   893	|libc      |See archLSB  |
   894	|----------+-------------|
   895	|proginterp|See archLSB  |
   896	+------------------------+
   897	
   898	These libraries will be in an implementation-dependent directory which the
   899	dynamic linker will search by default.
   900	
   901	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   902	
   903	How to Use this Standard
   904	
   905	The complete LSB specification is composed of this generic LSB specification
   906	and the supplemental processor-specific specification for a particular
   907	processor architecture. These two documents constitute a specification that
   908	should be used in conjunction with the publicly-available standards documents
   909	it references. The LSB enumerates the system components it includes, but
   910	descriptions of those components may be included entirely in the LSB, partly in
   911	the LSB and partly in other documents, or entirely in other reference
   912	documents.
   913	
   914	II. Base Libraries
   915	
   916	Table of Contents
   917	2. Libraries
   918	
   919	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   920	
   921	Chapter 2. Libraries
   922	
   923	An LSB-conforming implementation shall support some base libraries which
   924	provide interfaces for accessing the operating system, processor and other
   925	hardware in the system.
   926	
   927	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
   928

 11469	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11470	
 11471	Data Definitions for libstdcxx
 11472	
 11473	This section contains standard data definitions that describe system data.
 11474	These definitions are organized into groups that correspond to system headers.
 11475	This convention is used as a convenience for the reader, and does not imply the
 11476	existence of these headers, or their content.
 11477	
 11478	ISO C serves as the LSB reference programming language, and data definitions
 11479	are specified in ISO C format. The C language is used here as a convenient
 11480	notation. Using a C language description of these data objects does not
 11481	preclude their use by other programming languages.
 11482	
 11483	III. System Initialization
 11484	
 11485	Table of Contents
 11486	3. System Initialization
 11487	
 11488	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11489	
 11490	Chapter 3. System Initialization
 11491	
 11492	Cron Jobs
 11493	
 11494	Packages may not touch the configuration file /etc/crontab, nor may they modify
 11495	the files in /var/spool/cron/crontabs.
 11496	
 11497	If a package wants to install a job that has to be executed via cron, it shall
 11498	place a file in one of the following directories:
 11499	
 11500	/etc/cron.daily
 11501	/etc/cron.weekly
 11502	/etc/cron.monthly
 11503	
 11504	As these directory names say, the files within them are executed on a daily,
 11505	weekly, or monthly basis, respectively. See below for the rules concerning the
 11506	names of these files.
 11507	
 11508	 If a certain job has to be executed more frequently than daily, the package
 11509	shall install a file /etc/cron.d/cron-name tagged as configuration file. This
 11510	file uses the same syntax as /etc/crontab and is processed by cron
 11511	automatically.
 11512	
 11513	It is recommended that files installed in any of these directories be scripts
 11514	(shell scripts, Perl scripts, etc.) so that they may be modified by the local
 11515	system administrator. In addition, they must be registered as configuration
 11516	file.
 11517	
 11518	The scripts in these directories have to check, if all necessary programs are
 11519	installed before they try to execute them. Otherwise, problems will arise when
 11520	a package was removed (but not purged), since the configuration files are kept
 11521	on the system in this situation.
 11522	
 11523	To avoid namespace conflicts in the /etc/cron.* directories, the filenames used
 11524	by LSB-compliant packages in /etc/cron.daily, /etc/cron.weekly, /etc/
 11525	cron.monthly, or /etc/cron.d must come from a managed namespace. These
 11526	filenames may be assigned using one of the following methods:
 11527	
 11528	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
 11529	    character set [a-z0-9]. In order to avoid conflicts these cron script names
 11530	    must be reserved through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority
 11531	    (LANANA). Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
 11532	   
 11533	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
 11534	    should be encouraged reserve names from LANA, so that each distribution can
 11535	    use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other projects.
 11536	   
 11537	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
 11538	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
 11539	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
 11540	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
 11541	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'.
 11542	    I.e., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com", etc. The LSB provider name
 11543	    assigned by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
 11544	   
 11545	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
 11546	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
 11547	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
 11548	    namespace is highly discouraged.
 11549	   
 11550	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11551	
 11552	Init Script Actions
 11553	
 11554	Init files provided by LSB applications shall accept one argument, saying what
 11555	to do:
 11556	
 11557	start        start the service                                                 
 11558	stop         stop the service                                                  
 11559	restart      stop and restart the service if the service is already running,   
 11560	             otherwise start the service                                       
 11561	try-restart  restart the service if the service is already running             
 11562	reload       cause the configuration of the service to be reloaded without     
 11563	             actually stopping and restarting the service                      
 11564	force-reload cause the configuration to be reloaded if the service supports    
 11565	             this, otherwise restart the service                               
 11566	status       print the current status of the service                           
 11567	
 11568	The start, stop, restart, force-reload, and status commands must be supported
 11569	by all init files; the reload and the try-restart options are optional. Other
 11570	init script actions may be defined by the init script.
 11571	
 11572	Init files must ensure that they will behave sensibly if invoked with start
 11573	when the service is already running, or with stop when it isn't, and that they
 11574	don't kill unfortunately-named user processes. The best way to achieve this is
 11575	to use the init-script functions provided by /lib/lsb/init-functions.
 11576	
 11577	If a service reloads its configuration automatically (as in the case of cron,
 11578	for example), the reload option of the init file must behave as if the
 11579	configuration has been reloaded successfully.
 11580	
 11581	These executable files must not fail obscurely when the configuration files
 11582	remain but the package has been removed, as the default in [the packaging
 11583	system] is to leave configuration files on the system after the package has
 11584	been removed. Only when it is executed with the [purge] option will [the
 11585	packaging system] remove configuration files. Therefore, you should include a
 11586	test statement at the top of the file, like this:
 11587	test -f program-executed-later-in-file || exit 5
 11588	or take the equivalent action if the init file is not a shell script.
 11589	
 11590	If the status command is given, the init script will return the following exit
 11591	status codes.
 11592	
 11593	0       program is running or service is OK           
 11594	1       program is dead and /var/run pid file exists  
 11595	2       program is dead and /var/lock lock file exists
 11596	3       program is not running                        
 11597	4       program or service status is unknown          
 11598	5-99    reserved for future LSB use                   
 11599	100-149 reserved for distribution use                 
 11600	150-199 reserved for application use                  
 11601	200-254 reserved                                      
 11602	
 11603	In the case of init script commands other than "status" (i.e., "start", "stop",
 11604	"restart", "reload", and "force-reload"), the init script must return an exit
 11605	status of zero if the action described by the argument has been successful.
 11606	Otherwise, the exit status shall be non-zero, as defined below. In addition to
 11607	straightforward success, the following situations are also to be considered
 11608	successful:
 11609	
 11610	  * restarting a service (instead of reloading it) with the "force-reload"
 11611	    argument
 11612	   
 11613	  * running "start" on a service already running
 11614	   
 11615	  * running "stop" on a service already stopped or not running
 11616	   
 11617	  * running "restart" on a service already stopped or not running
 11618	   
 11619	 
 11620	
 11621	In case of an error, while processing any init script action except for
 11622	"status", the init script must print an error message and return one of the
 11623	following non-zero exit status codes.
 11624	
 11625	1       generic or unspecified error (current practice)
 11626	2       invalid or excess argument(s)                  
 11627	3       unimplemented feature (for example, "reload")  
 11628	4       user had insufficient privilege                
 11629	5       program is not installed                       
 11630	6       program is not configured                      
 11631	7       program is not running                         
 11632	8-99    reserved for future LSB use                    
 11633	100-149 reserved for distribution use                  
 11634	150-199 reserved for application use                   
 11635	200-254 reserved                                       
 11636	
 11637	Error and status messages should be printed with the logging functions such as
 11638	log_failure_msg and so on. Scripts may write to standard error or standard
 11639	output, but implementations need not present text written to standard error/
 11640	output to the user or do anything else with it.
 11641	
 11642	Since init files may be run manually by a system administrator with
 11643	non-standard environment variable values for PATH, USER, LOGNAME, etc. init
 11644	files must not depend on the values of these environment variables. They should
 11645	set them to some known/default values if they are needed. 
 11646	
 11647	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11648	
 11649	Comment conventions for init scripts
 11650	
 11651	LSB applications which need to execute script(s) at bootup and/or shutdown may
 11652	provide one or more init.d files. These files are installed by the
 11653	install_initd program described below, which copies it into a standard
 11654	directory and makes whatever other adjustments (creation of symlinks, creation
 11655	of entries in a database, etc.) are necessary so that the script can be run at
 11656	boot-time. [3]
 11657	
 11658	In the init.d file, information about the shell script shall be delimited by
 11659	the lines "### BEGIN INIT INFO" and "### END INIT INFO". These delimiter lines
 11660	may containg trailing whitespace, which shall be ignored. Inside this block
 11661	there shall be lines of the form "# {keyword}: [arg1] [arg2] ...". (All lines
 11662	inside this block start with a hash ('#') character in the first column, so
 11663	that shell treats them as comments.) There must be exactly one space character
 11664	between "#" and the keyword.[4] The following keywords, with their arguments
 11665	are defined in this specification:
 11666	
 11667	        # Provides: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 11668	        # Required-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 11669	        # Required-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 11670	        # Should-Start: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 11671	        # Should-Stop: boot_facility_1 [ boot_facility_2 ...]
 11672	        # Default-Start: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
 11673	        # Default-Stop: run_level_1 [ run_level_2 ...]
 11674	        # Short-Description: short_description
 11675	        # Description: multiline_description
 11676	
 11677	Additional keywords may be defined in future LSB specifications. Distributions
 11678	may define local extensions by using the prefix "X-[distribution name]" --- for
 11679	example, "X-RedHat-foobardecl", or "X-Debian-xyzzydecl".
 11680	
 11681	An init.d shell script may declare using the "Required-Start: " header that it
 11682	must not be run until certain boot facilities are provided. This information is
 11683	used by the installation tool or the boot-time boot-script execution facility
 11684	to assure that init scripts are run in the correct order. When an init script
 11685	is run with a "start" argument, the boot facility or facilities specified in
 11686	the "Provides" header shall be considered present, and hence init scripts which
 11687	require those boot facilities would then be eligble to be run. When an init
 11688	script is run with a "stop" argument, the boot facilities specified in the
 11689	"Provides" header are considered no longer present. There are naming
 11690	conventions for boot facilities and system facilities, as described in a
 11691	following section.
 11692	
 11693	Similarly, the "Required-Stop:" header defines which facilities must still be
 11694	available during the shutdown of that service. Hence, the init script system
 11695	should avoid stopping shell scripts which provide those facilities until this
 11696	shell script is stopped.
 11697	
 11698	The "Should-Start:" header defines which facilities if present should be
 11699	started before this service. This allows for weak dependencies which do not
 11700	cause the service to fail if a facility is not available. But may cause reduced
 11701	functionality of the service. Compliant applications should not rely on the
 11702	existence of this feature.
 11703	
 11704	The "Should-Stop:" header defines which facilities should be still available
 11705	during the shutdown of that service.
 11706	
 11707	The "Default-Start" and "Default-Stop" headers define which run levels should
 11708	by default run the script with a start or stop argument, respectively, to start
 11709	or stop the services controlled by the init script.
 11710	
 11711	The "Short-Description" and "Description" header fields are used to provide
 11712	text which describes the actions of the init script. The "short_description"
 11713	shall be a relatively short, pithy description of the init script, where as the
 11714	"multiline_description" can be a much longer piece of text that may span
 11715	mulitple lines. In a multiline description, each continuation line must begin
 11716	with a '#' followed by tab character or a '#' followed by at least two space
 11717	characters. The multiline description is terminated by the first line that does
 11718	not match this criteria.
 11719	
 11720	The comment conventions described in this session are only required for use by
 11721	LSB-compliant applications; system init scripts as provided by LSB-compliant
 11722	run-time environments are not required to use the scheme outlined here.
 11723	
 11724	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11725	
 11726	Installation and removal of init.d files
 11727	
 11728	An init.d file is installed by copying it into /etc/init.d (which may be a
 11729	symlink to another location). This can be done by the package installer. During
 11730	the package's postinstall script, the program "/usr/lib/lsb/install_initd"
 11731	configures the distribution's boot script system to call the package's init.d
 11732	file at the appropriate time.
 11733	
 11734	The install_initd program takes a single argument, the pathanme to the /etc/
 11735	init.d file. For example:
 11736	
 11737	        /usr/lib/lsb/install_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
 11738	
 11739	The install_initd program shall return an exit status of zero if the init.d
 11740	file has been successfully installed or if the the init.d file was already
 11741	installed. If the required boot facilities cannot be fulfilled an exit status
 11742	of one shall be returned and the init.d file shall not be installed.
 11743	
 11744	When a software package is removed, the package's preuninstall script shall
 11745	call /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd and pass the pathname to the /etc/init.d file.
 11746	The package manager is still responsible for removing the /etc/init.d file; the
 11747	remove_initd program is provided in case the distribution needs to clean up any
 11748	other modifications in the distribution's boot script system that might have
 11749	been made by the install_initd program. For example:
 11750	
 11751	        /usr/lib/lsb/remove_initd /etc/init.d/example.com-coffeed
 11752	
 11753	The remove_initd program shall return an exit status of zero if the init.d file
 11754	has been successfully removed or if the the init.d file is not installed. If
 11755	another init.d file which depends on a boot facility provided by this init.d
 11756	file is installed, an exit status of one shall be returned and the init.d file
 11757	shall remained installed.
 11758	
 11759	There should be a tool available to the user (e.g., RedHat's chkconfig) which
 11760	can be used by the system administrator to easily manipulate at which init
 11761	levels a particular init.d script is started or stopped. This specification
 11762	currently does not specify such an interface, however.
 11763	
 11764	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11765	
 11766	Run Levels
 11767	
 11768	 The following run levels are specified for use by the "Default-Start:" and
 11769	"Default-Stop:" specifiers as defined by the section Comment conventions for
 11770	init scripts>. Many LSB run-time environments commonly use these run level
 11771	definitions, and in the absence of other considerations, providers of run-time
 11772	environments are strongly encouraged to follow this convention to provide
 11773	consistency for system administrators who need to work with multiple
 11774	distributions. However, it is not required that LSB-compliant run-time
 11775	environments use these run levels; the distribution-provided install_initd
 11776	script may map the run levels specified below to whatever
 11777	distribution-specified run levels are most appropriate. 
 11778	
 11779	0 halt                                                    
 11780	1 single user mode                                        
 11781	2 multiuser with no network services exported             
 11782	3 normal/full multiuser                                   
 11783	4 reserved for local use, default is normal/full multiuser
 11784	5 multiuser with xdm or equivalent                        
 11785	6 reboot                                                  
 11786	
 11787	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11788	
 11789	Facility names
 11790	
 11791	Boot facilities are used to indicate dependencies in init scripts, as defined
 11792	in a previous section. Facility names that begin with a dollar sign ('$') are
 11793	system facility names, defined by the LSB, and MUST be provided by
 11794	distributions. [5] LSB applications must not provide facilities that begin with
 11795	a dollar sign. This document defines the following facility names:
 11796	
 11797	$local_fs  all local filesystems are mounted                                   
 11798	$network   low level networking (ethernet card; may imply PCMCIA running)      
 11799	$named     daemons which may provide hostname resolution (if present) are      
 11800	           running                                                             
 11801	$portmap   daemons providing SunRPC/ONCRPC portmapping service[6] (if present) 
 11802	           are running                                                         
 11803	$remote_fs all remote filesystems are mounted[7].                              
 11804	$syslog    system logger is operational                                        
 11805	$time      the system time has been set [8]                                    
 11806	
 11807	Other (non-system) facilities may be defined by other LSB applications. These
 11808	facilities shall be named using the same conventions defined for naming init.d
 11809	script names. Commonly, the facility provided by an LSB application init.d
 11810	script will have the same name as the name assigned to the init.d script.
 11811	
 11812	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11813	
 11814	Script names
 11815	
 11816	Since the init.d scripts must live in a single directory, they must come from a
 11817	single namespace. Three means of assigning names from this namespace are
 11818	available:
 11819	
 11820	  * Assigned namespace. This namespace consists of names which only use the
 11821	    character set [a-z0-9]. This space is desirable for scripts which system
 11822	    administrators may often wish to run manually: e.g., "/etc/init.d/named
 11823	    restart" In order to avoid conflicts these init.d names must be reserved
 11824	    through the Linux Assigned Names and Numbers Authority (LANANA).
 11825	    Information about the LANANA may be found at www.lanana.org.
 11826	   
 11827	    Commonly used names shall be reserved in advance; developers for projects
 11828	    should be encouraged to reserve names from LANANA, so that each
 11829	    distribution can use the same name, and to avoid conflicts with other
 11830	    projects.
 11831	   
 11832	  * Hierarchical namespace. This namespace consists of scripts names which look
 11833	    like this: [hier1]-[hier2]-...-[name], where name is again taken the
 11834	    character set [a-z0-9], and where there may be one or more [hier-n]
 11835	    components. [hier1] may either be an LSB provider name assigned by the
 11836	    LANANA, or it may be owners' DNS name in lower case, with at least one '.'
 11837	    (e.g., "debian.org", "staroffice.sun.com"). The LSB provider name assigned
 11838	    by LANANA must only consist of the ASCII characters [a-z0-9].
 11839	   
 11840	  * Reserved namespace. This namespace consists of script names which begin
 11841	    with the character '_', and is reserved for distribution use only. This
 11842	    namespace should be used for core packages only, and in general use of this
 11843	    namespace is highly discouraged.  
 11844	   
 11845	In general, if a package or some system function is likely to be used on
 11846	multiple systems, the package developers or the distribution SHOULD get a
 11847	registered name through LANANA, and distributions should strive to use the same
 11848	name whenever possible. For applications which may not be "core" or may not be
 11849	commonly installed, the hierarchical namespace may be more appropriate. An
 11850	advantage to the hierarchical namespace is that there is no need to consult
 11851	with the LANANA before obtaining an assigned name.
 11852	
 11853	Short names are highly desirable, since many system administrators like to use
 11854	them to manually start and stop services. Given this, they should be
 11855	standardized on a per-package basis. This is the rationale behind having a
 11856	LANANA organization to assign these names. The LANANA may be called upon to
 11857	handle other namespace issues, such as package/prerequisites naming (which is
 11858	essential to making prerequisites to work correctly).
 11859	
 11860	-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
 11861	
 11862	Init script Functions
 11863	
 11864	Each LSB-compliant init.d script must source the file /lib/lsb/init-functions.
 11865	This file must cause the following shell script commands to be defined. This
 11866	can be done either by adding a directory to the PATH variable which defines
 11867	these commands, or by defining sh aliases. While the distribution-provided
 11868	aliases may choose to use bash extensions (at the distribution's option), the
 11869	LSB init.d files themselves should only depend in /bin/sh features as defined
 11870	by POSIX.2.
 11871	
 11872	                This runs the specified program as a daemon. start_daemon will 
 11873	start_daemon    check to see if there is a program named "daemon" already      
 11874	[-f] [-n        running. If so, it will not start another copy of the daemon   
 11875	nicelevel] [-p  unless the -f option is given. The -n option specifies a nice  
 11876	pidfile]        level. See nice(1). start_daemon should return the LSB defined 
 11877	pathname [args] exit status codes. It shall return 0 if the program has been   
 11878	                successfully started or is running and not 0 otherwise.        
 11879	                This stops the specified program. The program is found using   
 11880	                the algorithm given by pidofproc. If a signal is specified,    
 11881	                using the -signal_name or -signal_number syntaxes specified by 
 11882	killproc [-p    the kill command, the program is sent that signal. Otherwise, a
 11883	pidfile]        SIGTERM followed by a SIGKILL after some number of seconds is  
 11884	pathname        sent. Compliant applications may use the basename instead of   
 11885	[signal]        the pathname. killproc should return the LSB defined exit      
 11886	                status codes. If called without a signal, it shall return 0 if 
 11887	                the program has been stopped or is not running and not 0       
 11888	                otherwise. If a signal is given, it shall return 0 only if the 
 11889	                program is running.                                            
 11890	                This function returns one or more pid(s) for a particular      
 11891	                daemon. If an entry is found in /var/run/basename.pid, then    
 11892	                that value is returned. If the -p option is used, the given    
 11893	                pidfile is used instead. Compliant applications may use the    
 11894	                basename instead of the pathname. Compliant implementations of 
 11895	                the LSB may attempt other mechanisms for determining the pid   
 11896	pidofproc [-p   (s), although this is not required (and not recommended, since 
 11897	pidfile]        a user can trick startup scripts by creating processes that    
 11898	pathname        appear to be system programs in the process list thus creating 
 11899	                a potential security exposure). Hence, LSB-complaint           
 11900	                applications who wish to use the pidofproc function in their   
 11901	                init scripts must store the pid in /var/run/basename.pid.      
 11902	                pidofproc should return the LSB defined exist status codes for 
 11903	                "status". It shall return 0 if the program is the process is   
 11904	                running and not 0 otherwise.                                   
 11905	log_success_msg This requests the distribution to print a success message. The 
 11906	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 11907	                is highly desirable.                                           
 11908	log_failure_msg This requests the distribution to print a failure message. The 
 11909	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 11910	                is highly desirable.                                           
 11911	log_warning_msg This requests the distribution to print a warning message. The 
 11912	"message"       message should be relatively short; no more than 60 characters 
 11913	                is highly desirable.                                           
 11914	
 11915	Notes
 11916

 11918
